<p>While a snake bite is usually not serious, it is safer to assume that a snake is venomous if a person has been bitten. A venomous bite is often painless. Depending on the snake, venom may cause local tissue destruction; it may block nerve impulses, causing breathing and the heart to stop; or, cause blood clotting (coagulation) and then internal bleeding.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="recognition">RECOGNITION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recognition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There may be:</p>
<ul>
<li>A pair of puncture marks—the bite may be painless</li>
<li>Severe pain, redness, and swelling at the bite</li>
<li>Nausea and vomiting</li>
<li>Disturbed vision</li>
<li>Increased salivation and sweating</li>
<li>Labored breathing; it may be stopped all together</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="aims">AIMS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aims" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>To prevent venom from spreading</li>
<li>To arrange urgent removal to the hospital</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-to-do">WHAT TO DO<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-to-do" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Help the casualty lie down, with head and shoulders raised. Reassure the casualty and advise her not to move the bitten limb to prevent venom from spreading. Call 911 for emergency help.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>If you have been properly trained, consider wrapping a pressure bandage around the entire length of the limb that was bitten. The bandage should be comfortably snug but loose enough to allow a finger to be slipped under it</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Whether or not it is wrapped, the bitten limb should be immobilized with a splint to prevent the casualty from bending it. Keep the limb below the level of the heart.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Monitor and record vital signs while waiting for emergency help. The casualty must remain still, and should be taken to the hospital as soon as possible.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="foreign-bodies">FOREIGN BODIES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#foreign-bodies" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="swallowed-foreign-objects">Swallowed foreign objects<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#swallowed-foreign-objects" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Children may put small items in their mouths when playing. An adult may swallow a bone by mistake or ingest unlikely objects on purpose. Most objects will pass through the digestive system, but some can cause a blockage or perforation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="caution">CAUTION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#caution" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Do not let the casualty make himself vomit because the object could damage the esophagus.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="aim">AIM<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aim" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>To obtain medical advice as soon as possible</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-to-do">WHAT TO DO<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-to-do" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>a. Reassure the casualty and find out what he swallowed.
b. Seek medical advice</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="foreign-object-in-the-eye">FOREIGN OBJECT IN THE EYE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#foreign-object-in-the-eye" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Foreign objects such as grit, a loose eyelash, or a contact lens that are floating on the surface of the eye can be easily rinsed out. However, you must not attempt to remove anything that sticks to the eye or penetrates the eyeball because this may damage the eye.</p>
<p>Instead, make sure that the casualty receives urgent medical attention.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="recognition">RECOGNITION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recognition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There may be:</p>
<ul>
<li>Blurred vision</li>
<li>Pain or discomfort</li>
<li>Redness and watering of the eye</li>
<li>Eyelids held tight in spasm</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="aim">AIM<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aim" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>To prevent injury to the eye</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-to-do">WHAT TO DO<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-to-do" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Advise the casualty not to rub her eye. Ask her to sit down facing a light</li>
<li>Stand besides, or just behind, the casualty. Gently separate her eyelids with your thumbs or finger and thumb. Ask her to look right, left, up, and down. Examine every part of her eye as she does this.</li>
<li>If you can see a foreign object on the white of the eye, wash it out by pouring clean water from a glass or pitcher, or by using a sterile eyewash if you have one. Put a towel around the casualty's shoulders. Hold her eye open and pour the water from the inner corner so that it drains onto the towel.</li>
<li>If this is unsuccessful, try lifting the object off with a moist swab or the damp corner of a clean handkerchief or tissue. If you still cannot remove the object, seek medical help</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="foreign-object-in-the-ear">FOREIGN OBJECT IN THE EAR<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#foreign-object-in-the-ear" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>If a foreign object becomes lodged in the ear, it may cause temporary loss of hearing by blocking the ear canal. In some cases, a foreign</p>
<p>object may damage the eardrum. Young children frequently push objects into their ears. The tips of cotton swabs are often left in the ear. Insects can fly or crawl into the ear and may cause distress</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="aims">AIMS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aims" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>To prevent injury to the ear</li>
<li>To remove a trapped insect</li>
<li>To arrange transportation to the hospital if a foreign object is lodged in the ear</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-to-do">WHAT TO DO<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-to-do" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Arrange to take or send the casualty to the hospital. Do not try to remove a lodged foreign object yourself.</li>
<li>Reassure the casualty during the journey or until medical help arrives</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="special-case-insect-inside-the-ear">SPECIAL CASE INSECT INSIDE THE EAR<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#special-case-insect-inside-the-ear" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Reassure the casualty and ask him to sit down. Support his head, with the affected ear uppermost. Gently flood the ear with tepid water; the insect should float out. If this flooding does not remove the insect, seek medical help</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="foreign-object-in-the-nose">FOREIGN OBJECT IN THE NOSE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#foreign-object-in-the-nose" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Young children may push small objects up their noses. Objects can block the nose and cause infection. If the object is sharp it can damage the tissues, and "button" batteries can cause burns and bleeding. Do not try to remove a foreign object; you may cause injury or push it farther into the airway</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="recognition">RECOGNITION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recognition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There may be:</p>
<p>&gt; Difficult or noisy breathing through the nose
&gt; Swelling of the nose
&gt; Smelly or blood-stained discharge, indicating that an object may have been lodged for some time</p>
<p><strong>AIM</strong></p>
<p>To arrange transportation to the hospital</p>
<p><strong>WHAT TO DO</strong></p>
<ol>
<li>Try to keep the casualty quiet and calm. Tell him to breathe through his mouth at a normal rate. Advise him not to poke inside his nose to try to remove the object himself. Reassure the casualty during the journey or until medical help arrives.</li>
<li>Arrange to take or send the casualty to the hospital, so that it can be safely removed by medical staff</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>CHILD BIRTH</strong></p>
<p>Childbirth is a natural and often lengthy process that normally occurs at about the 40th week of pregnancy. There is usually plenty of time to get a woman to the hospital, or get help to her, before the baby arrives. Most pregnant women are aware of what happens during childbirth, but a woman who goes into labor unexpectedly or early may be very anxious. You will need to reassure her and make her comfortable. Miscarriage, however, is potentially serious because there is a risk of severe bleeding. A woman who is miscarrying needs urgent medical help (p.128). There are three distinct stages to childbirth. In the first stage, the baby gets into position for the birth. The baby is born in the second stage, and in the third stage, the afterbirth (placenta and umbilical cord) is delivered.</p>
<p><strong>AIMS</strong></p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>To obtain medical help or arrange for the woman to be taken to the hospital</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>To ensure privacy, reassure the woman and make her comfortable</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>To prevent infection in the mother, baby, and yourself</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>To care for the baby during and after deliver</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="stages-of-labor">STAGES OF LABOR<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#stages-of-labor" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="first-stage">First stage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#first-stage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>In this stage, a woman's body begins to experience contractions, which, together with the pressure of the baby's head, cause the cervix (neck of the uterus/womb) to open. The contractions become stronger and more frequent until the cervix is fully dilated (open)—about 4 in (10 cm)—and ready for the baby to be born. During this first stage, the mucous plug that protects the uterus from infection is expelled and the amniotic fluid surrounding the baby leaks out from the vagina. This stage can take several hours for a first baby but is normally shorter in any subsequent pregnancies.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="second-stage">Second stage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#second-stage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Once the cervix is fully dilated, the baby's head will press down on the mother's pelvic floor, triggering a strong urge to push. The birth canal (vagina) stretches as the baby travels through it. The baby's head normally emerges first, and the body is delivered soon afterward. This stage of labor normally lasts about an hour</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="third-stage">Third stage<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#third-stage" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>About 10–30 minutes after the baby is born, the placenta (the organ that nourishes the unborn baby) and the umbilical cord will be expelled from the uterus. The uterus begins to contract again,</p>
<p>pushing the placenta out, then it closes down the area where it was attached; this reduces the bleeding.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="emergency-childbirth">EMERGENCY CHILDBIRTH<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#emergency-childbirth" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In the rare event of a baby arriving quickly, you should not try to "deliver" the baby; the birth will happen naturally without intervention. Your role is to comfort and listen to the wishes of the mother and care for her and her baby.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="caution">Caution<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#caution" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>I. Do not give the mother anything to eat because there is a risk that she may vomit. If she is thirsty give her sips of water.
II. Do not pull on the baby's head or shoulders during delivery.
III. If the umbilical cord is wrapped around the baby's neck as he is born, check that it is loose, and then very carefully ease it over the baby's head to prevent strangulation.
IV. If a newborn baby does not cry, open the airway and check breathing (Unconscious infant pp. 80-83). Do not slap the baby.
V. Do not pull or cut the umbilical cord, even when the placenta has been delivered.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-to-do">WHAT TO DO<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-to-do" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>I. Call 911 for emergency help. Give the ambulance control details of the stage the mother has reached, the length of each contraction and the intervals between them. Call the mother's midwife too if she request it.
II. During the first stage, help her sit or kneel on the floor in a comfortable position. Support her with cushions or let her move around. Stay calm, and encourage her to breathe deeply during her contractions.
III. Massage her lower back gently using the heel of your hand. She may find having her face and hands wiped soothing, or you can spray her face with cool water and give her ice cubes to suck</p>
<p>IV. When the second stage starts, the mother will want to push. Make sure the surroundings are as clean as possible to reduce the risk of infection. The mother should remove any items of clothing that could interfere with the birth. Put clean sheets or towels under the woman; she may also want to be covered. Encourage her to stay as upright as possible.</p>
<p>V. As the baby is born, handle him carefully—newborns are very slippery. Make sure he is breathing, wrap him in a clean cloth, towel, or blanket, and place him between his mother’s legs so he is on the same level as the afterbirth.</p>
<p>VI. As the third stage begins, reassure the mother. Support her as she delivers the afterbirth; do not cut the cord. Keep the placenta and the umbilical cord intact because the doctor or ambulance crew need to check that it is complete. If bleeding or pain is severe, treat for shock. Help the mother lie down and raise her legs.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="convulsions">CONVULSIONS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#convulsions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A seizure—also called a convulsion—consists of involuntary contractions of many of the muscles in the body. The condition is due to a disturbance in the electrical activity of the brain. Seizures usually result in loss or impairment of consciousness. The most common cause is epilepsy. Other causes include head injury, some brain-damaging diseases, shortage of oxygen or glucose in the brain, and the intake of certain poisons, including alcohol or drugs.</p>
<p>Epileptic seizures result from recurrent, major disturbances of brain activity. These seizures can be sudden and dramatic. Just before a seizure, a casualty may have a brief warning period (aura) with, for example, a strange feeling or a particular smell or taste.</p>
<p>No matter what the cause of the seizure, care must always include maintaining an open, clear airway and a monitoring of the casualty's vital signs—level of response, breathing, and pulse. You will also need to protect the casualty from further harm during a seizure and arrange appropriate aftercare once he has recovered.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="special-case-absence-seizures">SPECIAL CASE; ABSENCE SEIZURES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#special-case-absence-seizures" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Some people experience a mild form of epilepsy known as absence seizures, during which they appear distant and unaware of their surroundings. These seizures tend to affect children more than adults, and a more severe seizure with convulsions may follow. A casualty may suddenly "switch off" and stare blankly ahead. You may notice slight or localized twitching or jerking of the lips, eyelids, head, or limbs and/or odd "automatic" movements, such as lip-smacking or making noises. If a casualty has an absence seizure:</p>
<ul>
<li>Help him sit down in a quiet place</li>
<li>Remove any potentially dangerous items such as hot drinks and sharp objects</li>
<li>Talk to him in a calm and reassuring way and stay with him until he has fully recovered</li>
<li>Advise him to seek medical advice if he is unaware of his condition or does not fully recover.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="aims">AIMS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aims" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>To protect the casualty from injury during the seizure</li>
<li>To care for the casualty when consciousness is regained and arrange removal to the hospital if necessary</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="recognition">RECOGNITION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recognition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In epilepsy, the following sequence is common:</p>
<p>I. Sudden loss of consciousness
II. Casualty becomes rigid and arches his back</p>
<p>III. Breathing may be noisy and become difficult—the lips may show a grayblue tinge (cyanosis)
IV. Convulsive movements begin
V. Saliva may appear at the mouth and may be bloodstained if the lips or tongue have been bitten
VI. Possible loss of bladder or bowel control
VII. Muscles relax and breathing becomes normal; the casualty recovers consciousness, usually within a few minutes. He may feel dazed, or act strangely. He may be unaware of his actions
VIII. After a seizure, the casualty may feel tired and fall into a deep sleep</p>
<p><strong>CAUTION</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Do not move the casualty unless he is in immediate danger or is vomiting.</li>
<li>Do not put anything in his mouth or attempt to restrain him during a seizure.</li>
</ul>
<p>Call 911 for emergency help if:</p>
<ul>
<li>The casualty is having repeated seizures or having his first seizure.</li>
<li>The casualty is not aware of any reason for the seizure.</li>
<li>The seizure continues for more than five minutes.</li>
<li>The casualty is unconscious for more than ten minutes.</li>
<li>The casualty has sustained an injury to another part of the body.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>What to do</strong></p>
<ol>
<li>Make space around the casualty, and ask bystanders to move away. Remove potentially dangerous items, such as hot drinks and sharp objects. Note the time that the seizure started.</li>
<li>Protect the casualty's head from objects nearby; place soft padding such as rolled towels underneath or around his neck if possible. Loosen tight clothing around his neck if necessary</li>
<li>when the convulsive movements have ceased, open the casualty's airway and check breathing. If he is breathing, place him in the recovery position. Monitor and record his vital</li>
</ol>
<p>signs—level of response, breathing, and pulse until he recovers. Make a note of how long the seizure lasted.</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>Monitor and record his vital signs—level of response, breathing, and pulse until he recovers. Make a note of how long the seizure lasted.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="seizures-in-children">SEIZURES IN CHILDREN<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#seizures-in-children" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In young children, seizures—sometimes called convulsions—are most often the result of a raised body temperature associated with a throat or ear infection or other infections. This type of seizure, also known as a febrile seizure, occurs because the electrical systems in the brain are not mature enough to deal with the body's high temperature.</p>
<p>Although seizures can be alarming, they are rarely dangerous if properly dealt with. However, you should always seek medical advice for the child to rule out any serious underlying condition.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="recognition">RECOGNITION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recognition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Loss of impaired response</li>
<li>Vigorous shaking, with clenched fists and an arched back.</li>
</ul>
<p>There may also be:</p>
<ul>
<li>Obvious signs of fever: hot, flushed skin and perhaps sweating</li>
<li>Twitching of the face and squinting, fixed or upturned eyes</li>
<li>Breath-holding, with red, “puffy” face and neck and drooling</li>
<li>Possible vomiting</li>
<li>Loss of bowel or bladder control</li>
<li>Loss of or impaired consciousness</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="aims">AIMS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aims" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>To protect the child from injury during the seizure</li>
<li>To cool the child</li>
</ul>
<p>To reassure the parents
To arrange removal to the hospital</p>
<p><strong>Caution</strong></p>
<p>Do not over- or under-dress a child with fever; do not sponge a child to cool her because there is a risk of overcooling.</p>
<p><strong>What to do</strong></p>
<ol>
<li>Place pillows or soft padding around the child so that even violent movement will not result in injury. Do not restrain the child in any way.</li>
<li>If the child's seizure was caused by a fever, cool him by removing any bedding and clothes, for example T-shirt or pajama top; you may have to wait until the seizure stops. Ensure a good supply of fresh air (but do not overcool the child).</li>
<li>Once the seizures have stopped, maintain an open airway by placing the casualty in the recovery position. Call 911 for emergency help</li>
<li>Reassure the child as well as the parents or caregiver. Monitor and record vital signs—level of response, breathing, and pulse until emergency help arrives</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>POISONING</strong></p>
<p>A poison (toxin) is a substance that, if taken into or absorbed into the body in sufficient quantity, can cause either temporary or permanent damage. Poisons can be swallowed, absorbed through the skin, inhaled, splashed into the eyes, or injected. Once in the body, they may enter the bloodstream and be carried swiftly to all organs and tissues. Signs and symptoms of poisoning vary with the poison. They may develop quickly or over a number of days. Vomiting is common, especially when the poison has been ingested. Inhaled poisons often cause breathing difficulties.</p>
<p><strong>Types of poison</strong></p>
<p>Some poisons are man-made—for example, chemicals and drugs—and these are found in the home as well as in industry.</p>
<p>Other poisons occur in nature: for example, plants produce poisons that may irritate the skin or cause more serious symptoms if ingested, and various insects and creatures produce venom in their bites and stings. Contamination of food by bacteria may result in food poisoning—one of the most common forms of poisoning</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>ROUTE OF ENTRY INTO THE BODY</th><th>Poison</th><th>Possible effects</th><th>Action</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Swallowed</td><td>Drugs and alcohol</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Cleaning products</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Home improvement and gardening products</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Plant poisons</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Bacterial food poisons</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Viral food poisons</td><td>Nausea and vomiting</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Abdominal pain</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Seizures</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Irregular, or fast or slow, heartbeat</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Impaired level of consciousness</td><td>Monitor casualty</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Call emergency help</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Start CPR if necessary</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Use a face mask to protect yourself if you need to give rescue breaths</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Absorbed through the skin</td><td>■ Cleaning products</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>■ Home improvement and gardening products</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>■ Industrial poisons</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>■ Plant poisons</td><td>■ Pain</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>■ Swelling</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>■ Rash</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>■ Redness</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>■ Itching</td><td>■ Remove contaminated clothing</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>■ Wash with cold water for 20 minutes</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>■ Seek medical help</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>■ Start CPR if necessary</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Inhaled</td><td>■ Fumes of cleaning and</td><td>■ Difficulty breathing</td><td>■ Help casualty into fresh air</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>SWALLOWED POISON</p>
<p>|   | construction products

| --- | --- | --- | --- |

<p>Chemicals that are swallowed may harm the digestive tract, or cause more widespread damage if they enter the bloodstream and are transported to other parts of the body. Hazardous chemicals include household substances such as bleach and paint stripper, which are poisonous or corrosive if swallowed.</p>
<p>Drugs, both prescribed or those bought over the counter, can also be harmful if an overdose is taken. Some plants and their berries can also be poisonous.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="caution">CAUTION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#caution" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Never attempt to induce vomiting.</li>
<li>If a casualty is contaminated with chemicals, wear protective gloves, and goggles or a mask.</li>
<li>If the casualty is unconscious and is not breathing (or just gasping), begin CPR with chest compressions.</li>
<li>If there are any chemicals on the casualty's mouth, use a face shield or pocket mask (adult rescue breaths).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="recognition">RECOGNITION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recognition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>History of ingestion/exposure</li>
</ul>
<p>Depending on what has been swallowed, there may be:</p>
<ul>
<li>Vomiting, sometimes bloodstained, later diarrhea</li>
<li>Cramping abdominal pains</li>
<li>Pain or a burning sensation</li>
<li>Empty containers in the vicinity</li>
<li>Impaired consciousness</li>
<li>Seizures</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="aims">AIMS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aims" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>To maintain an open airway, breathing, and circulation</li>
<li>To remove any contaminated clothing</li>
<li>To identify the poison</li>
<li>To arrange urgent removal to the hospital</li>
</ul>
<p>WHAT TO DO</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>If the casualty is conscious, ask her what she has swallowed, and if possible how much and when. Look for clues—for example, poisonous plants, berries or empty containers. Try to reassure her.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>call 911 for emergency help. Give the ambulance control as much information as possible about the poison. This information will help the medical team treat the casualty.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Monitor and record the casualty's vital signs while waiting for help to arrive. Keep samples of any vomited material. Give these samples, containers, and any others clues to the emergency services.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>SPECIAL CASE IF LIPS ARE BURNED</p>
<p>If the casualty's lips are burned by corrosive substances, give him frequent sips of cold milk or water while waiting for help to arrive.</p>
<p>DRUG POISONING</p>
<p>Poisoning can result from an overdose of prescribed drugs, or drugs that are bought over the counter. It can also be caused by drug abuse or drug interaction. The effects vary depending on the type of drug and how it is taken (below). When you call the emergency services, give as much information as possible. While waiting for help to arrive, look for containers that might help you identify the drug.</p>
<p>CAUTION</p>
<p>Do not induce vomiting.</p>
<p>If the casualty loses consciousness and is not breathing (or is just gasping), begin CPR with chest compressions.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="aims">AIMS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aims" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>To maintain breathing and circulation
To arrange removal to the hospital</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="what-to-do">WHAT TO DO<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-to-do" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>If the casualty is responding help him into a comfortable position and ask him what he has taken.</li>
</ol>
<p>Reassure him while you talk to him.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Call 911 for emergency help. Tell the Ambulance control you suspect drugpoisoning. Monitor</li>
</ol>
<p>and record vital signs—level of response, breathing and pulse while waiting.</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>Keep samples of any vomited material. Look for evidence that might help identify the drug, such as empty containers. Give these samples and containers to the ambulance personnel.</li>
</ol>
<table><thead><tr><th>Category</th><th>Drug</th><th>Effects of poisoning</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Painkillers</td><td>Aspirin (swallowed)</td><td>■ Upper abdominal pain, nausea and vomiting ■ Ringing in the ears ■</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>Paracetamol (swallowed)</td><td>“Sighing” when breathing ■ Confusion and delirium ■ Dizziness ■ Little effect at first, but abdominal pain, nausea, and vomiting may develop ■ Irreversible liver damage may occur within three days (alcohol and malnourishment increase the risk)</td></tr></tbody></table>
<table><thead><tr><th>Nervous system depressants and traquilisers</th><th>Barbiturates and benzodiazepines (swallowed)</th><th>■ Lethargy and sleepiness, leading to unconsciousness ■ Shallow breathing ■ Weak, irregular or abnormally slow or fast pulse</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Stimulants and hallucinogens</td><td>Amphetamines (including ecstasy) and LSD (Swallowed) Cocaine (inhaled or injected) Legal highs</td><td>■ Excitable, hyperactive behavior, agitation ■ Sweating ■ Tremor of the hands ■ Hallucinations, in which the casualty may claim to “hear voices” or “see things” ■ Dilated pupils</td></tr><tr><td>Narcotics</td><td>Morphine, heroine (commonly injected)</td><td>■ Small pupils ■ Sluggishness and confusion, possibly leading to unconsciousness ■ Slow, shallow breathing, which may stop altogether ■ Needle marks, which may be infected</td></tr><tr><td>Solvents</td><td>Glue, lighter fuel (inhaled)</td><td>■ Nausea and vomiting ■ Headaches ■ Hallucinations ■ Possibly, unconsciousness ■ Rarely, cardiac arrest</td></tr><tr><td>Anaesthetic</td><td>Ketamine</td><td>■ Drowsiness ■ Shallow breathing ■ Hallucinations</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="alcohol-poisoning">ALCOHOL POISONING<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#alcohol-poisoning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Alcohol is a drug that depresses the activity of the central nervous system—in particular, the brain. Prolonged or excessive intake of alcohol can severely impair all physical and mental functions, and the person may sink into deep unconsciousness.</p>
<p>There are other risks to a casualty from alcohol poisoning. For example: an unconscious casualty may inhale and choke on vomit; alcohol widens (dilates) the blood vessels so the body loses heat, and</p>
<p>hypothermia may develop. A casualty who smells of alcohol may be misdiagnosed and not receive appropriate treatment for an underlying cause of unconsciousness, such as a head injury, stroke, heart attack, or hypoglycemia</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="recognition">RECOGNITION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recognition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There may be:</p>
<ul>
<li>A strong smell of alcohol</li>
<li>Empty bottles or cans</li>
<li>Impaired consciousness: the casualty may respond if roused, but will quickly relapse</li>
<li>Flushed and moist face</li>
<li>Deep, noisy breathing</li>
<li>Full, bounding pulse</li>
</ul>
<p>In the later stages:</p>
<ul>
<li>Shallow breathing</li>
<li>Weak, rapid pulse</li>
<li>Dilated pupils that react poorly</li>
<li>No response</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="aim">AIM<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aim" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>To maintain an open airway</li>
<li>To assess for other conditions</li>
<li>To seek medical help if necessary</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-to-do">WHAT TO DO<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-to-do" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Cover the casualty with a coat or blanket to protect him from the cold and reassure him.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Assess the casualty for any injuries, especially head injuries, or other medical conditions.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Monitor and record vital signs—level of response, pulse, and breathing until the casualty recovers or is placed in the care of a responsible person. If you are in any doubt about the casualty's condition, call for emergency help</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>CAUTION</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Do not induce vomiting.</li>
<li>If the casualty loses consciousness, open the airway. If breathing stops, begin CPR with chest compressions.</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>BURNS</strong></p>
<p><strong>Types of burns</strong></p>
<ul>
<li>Dry burn</li>
<li>Scald</li>
<li>Electrical burn</li>
<li>Cold injury</li>
<li>Chemical burn</li>
<li>Radiation burn</li>
</ul>
<p><strong>Classification of burns</strong></p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>According to surface area
Uses a simple formula, the rule of nines</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>According to depth of burns</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p><strong>Adult</strong></p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Anterior head</th><th>9%</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Posterior head</td><td>9%</td></tr><tr><td>Anterior torso</td><td>18%</td></tr><tr><td>Posterior torso</td><td>18%</td></tr></tbody></table>
<table><thead><tr><th>Anterior leg each</th><th>9%</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Posterior leg each</td><td>9%</td></tr><tr><td>Anterior arm each</td><td>4.5%</td></tr><tr><td>Posterior arm each</td><td>4.5%</td></tr><tr><td>Genitalia/perineum</td><td>1%</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>According to depth of burns</p>
<ol>
<li>Superficial burn</li>
</ol>
<p>Involves only the outermost layer of the skin. Characterized by redness, swelling and tenderness</p>
<p>Heals well if first aid is given promptly.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>Partial-thickness burn</li>
</ol>
<p>any one percent burn affecting layers of the epidermis giving rise to rawness and blisters.</p>
<p>Can heal well but if but if they affect very large areas (over 60%), can be fatal</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>Full-thickness burns</li>
</ol>
<p>All the three layers of the skin are burnt. Damage to nerves, fat tissue and muscles present</p>
<p>The skin looks waxy, pale or charred. Urgent medical aid is essential</p>
<p>Management of minor burns and scalds</p>
<p>Aims</p>
<ul>
<li>To stop the burning</li>
<li>To relieve pain and swelling</li>
<li>To minimize the risk of infection</li>
</ul>
<p>What to do</p>
<ul>
<li>Flood the injured part with cold water for at least ten minutes to stop the burning and relieve pain. If water is not available any cold harmless liquid such as milk or canned drinks will do</li>
<li>Remove any jewellery, watches, belts or constricting clothing from the injured area before it begins to swell</li>
<li>Cover the area with a sterile dressing, or any clean, non-fluffy material and bandage loosely in place. A plastic bag or some kitchen film make good temporary covering</li>
<li>Do not break blisters</li>
<li>Do not apply adhesive dressings or adhesive tape to the skin: the burn may be more extensive</li>
<li>Do not apply lotions, ointments or fats to the injury. They further damage the tissue and increase the risk of infection</li>
</ul>
<p>Severe burns and scalds</p>
<p>Aims of treatment</p>
<ul>
<li>To stop the burning and relieve pain</li>
<li>To maintain an open airway</li>
<li>To treat associated injuries</li>
<li>To minimize the risk of infection</li>
<li>To arrange removal to hospital</li>
<li>To gather relevant information for the emergency services</li>
</ul>
<p>Management</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Lay the casualty down. Protect the burned area from contact with the ground</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Douse the burn with plenty of cold liquid. Thorough cooling may take at least ten minutes.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>While cooling the burn, watch for signs of DIB and be ready to resuscitate if necessary</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Remove any rings, watches, belts, shoes or shouldering clothing from the injured area before it begins to swell</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Cover the injury with a sterile dressing to protect it from infection. If the burn is on the face, don't cover it. Keep cooling a facial injury with water to relieve pain until help arrives</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Gather and record details of the casualty's injury, circumstances and potential hazards such as gas</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>While waiting for help, reassure the casualty and treat for shock</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="burns-to-the-airway">Burns to the Airway<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#burns-to-the-airway" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="signs-and-symptoms">Signs and symptoms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#signs-and-symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Soot around the nose and mouth</li>
<li>Singeing of the nasal hairs</li>
<li>Redness, swelling or actual burning of the tongue</li>
<li>Damaged skin around the mouth</li>
<li>Hoarseness of the voice</li>
<li>Breathing difficulties</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="aims-and-treatment">Aims and treatment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aims-and-treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>To obtain specialist medical aid as quickly as possible</li>
<li>To maintain an open airway</li>
<li>Call for help</li>
<li>Loosen tight clothing around his neck, give oxygen if available</li>
<li>Reassure the casualty for him/her to stay calm</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="electrical-burns">Electrical burns<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#electrical-burns" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Causes</p>
<ul>
<li>Lightning strike</li>
<li>Low or high voltage current</li>
</ul>
<p>Aims of treatment</p>
<ul>
<li>To treat the burn and the shock</li>
<li>To arrange removal of the casualty to hospital</li>
</ul>
<p>Treatment</p>
<ul>
<li>Make sure that contact with the electrical source is broken</li>
<li>If unconscious, resuscitate if necessary</li>
<li>Flood the sites of injury with plenty of cold water to cool the burns and cut away any burnt clothing if necessary</li>
<li>Place a sterile dressing, a clean folded triangular bandage or some clean non-fluffy material over the burns</li>
<li>Call for help</li>
<li>Reassure the casualty and treat for shock</li>
</ul>
<p>Chemical burns
If possible note the name or brand name of the substance</p>
<p>Sign and symptoms</p>
<ul>
<li>Evidence of chemicals in the vicinity</li>
<li>Intense stinging pain</li>
<li>Later, discoloration, blistering, peeling and swelling of the affected area</li>
</ul>
<p>Aims</p>
<ol>
<li>To disperse the harmful chemical</li>
<li>To arrange transport to hospital</li>
<li>To make the area safe and inform relevant Authority</li>
</ol>
<p>What to do</p>
<ul>
<li>Make the area safe. Ventilate the area, seal the chemical container. Remove the casualty from the area if necessary</li>
</ul>
<p>&gt; Flood the affected area with water to disperse the chemical and to stop the burning. Do this for at least 20 minutes
&gt; Gently remove contaminated clothing while flooding the injury
&gt; Take or send casualty to hospital. Note and pass any information about the chemical to the medical personnel. If at work place notify the local safety officer or emergency services</p>
<p>Chemical burns to the eye</p>
<p>Chemicals result in scarring and even blindness</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="signs-and-symptoms">Signs and symptoms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#signs-and-symptoms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>intense pain in the eye</li>
<li>Inability to open the injured eye</li>
<li>Redness and swelling round the eye</li>
<li>Copious watering of the eye</li>
<li>Evidence of chemical substances or containers in the immediate area</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-to-do">What to do<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-to-do" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Put on protective gloves. Hold the casualty's affected eye under gently running cold water for at least ten minutes. Irrigate the eyelid thoroughly both inside and out; if the casualty's eye is shut in a spasm of pain, gently, but firmly, try to pull the eyelid open</li>
<li>Make sure that contaminated water does not splash the uninjured eye. You may find it easier to pour the water over the eye using an eye irrigator or a glass</li>
<li>Ask the casualty to hold a sterile eye pad over the injured eye. If some time elapse elapses before the casualty receives medical attention, bandage the pad loosely in position</li>
<li>Arrange casualty's removal to hospital</li>
</ul>
<p>Sunburn</p>
<p>Aims</p>
<ul>
<li>To move the casualty out of the sun</li>
<li>To relieve discomfort and pain</li>
</ul>
<p>What to do</p>
<ol>
<li>Cover the casualty's skin with light clothing or towel. Help her into the shade or preferably indoors</li>
<li>Cool her skin by sponging with cold water or by soaking the affected area in a cold water bath for ten minutes. If there is extensive blistering or other skin damage, seek medical advice</li>
<li>Give her frequent sips of cold water. If the burns are mild, calamine or an after-sun preparation may sooth them</li>
</ol>
<p>HYPOTHERMIA</p>
<p>This condition develops when the body temperature falls below 95°F (35°C). T. Hypothermia can be caused by prolonged exposure to cold. Hypothermia may also develop indoors in poorly heated houses. Elderly people, infants, homeless people, and those who are thin and frail are particularly vulnerable. Lack of activity, chronic illness and fatigue all increase the risk; alcohol and drugs can exacerbate the condition</p>
<p>RECOGNITION</p>
<p>As hypothermia develops, there may be:</p>
<p>&gt; Shivering, and cold, pale, dry skin
&gt; Apathy, disorientation, or irrational behavior
&gt; Lethargy or impaired consciousness
&gt; Slow and shallow breathing
&gt; Slow and weakening pulse. In extreme cases, the heart may stop</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="aims">AIMS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aims" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>&gt; To prevent the casualty from losing more body heat
&gt; To rewarm the casualty quickly
&gt; To obtain emergency help if necessary</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-to-do">What to do<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-to-do" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Take the casualty to a sheltered place as quickly as possible. Shield the casualty from the wind.</li>
<li>Remove and replace any wet clothing if possible; do not give him your clothes. Make sure his head is covered.</li>
<li>Protect the casualty from the ground. Lay him on a thick layer of dry insulating material, such as pine branches. Put him in a dry sleeping bag and/or cover him with blankets or newspapers. Wrap him in a plastic or foil survival bag, if available. You can shelter and warm him with your body.</li>
<li>Call 911 or send for emergency help. Ideally, two people should go for help and stay together if you are in a remote area. It is important that you do not leave the casualty by himself; someone must remain with him at all times.</li>
<li>To help rewarm a casualty who is conscious, give him warm drinks and high-energy foods such as chocolate, if available.</li>
<li>Monitor and record the casualty's vital signs—level of response, breathing, pulse, and temperature—while waiting for help to arrive</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="frostbite">FROSTBITE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#frostbite" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>With this condition, the tissues of the extremities—usually the fingers and toes—freeze due to low temperatures. In severe cases, this freezing can lead to permanent loss of sensation and, eventually, tissue death and gangrene as the blood vessels and soft tissues become permanently damaged. Frostbite usually occurs in freezing or cold and windy conditions. People who cannot move around to increase their circulation are particularly susceptible. In many cases, frostbite is accompanied by hypothermia, and this should be treated accordingly.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="recognition">RECOGNITION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recognition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There may be:</p>
<ol>
<li>At first, "pins-and-needles"</li>
<li>Paleness (pallor) followed by numbness</li>
<li>Hardening and stiffening of the skin</li>
<li>A color change of the skin of the affected area: first white, then mottled and blue. On recovery, the skin may be red, hot, painful, and blistered. Where gangrene occurs, the tissue may become black due to loss of blood supply</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="aims">AIMS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aims" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>To warm the affected area slowly to prevent further tissue damage</li>
<li>To arrange transportation to the hospital</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="what-to-do">What to do<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-to-do" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Advise the casualty to put his hands, if affected, in his armpits. Move the casualty into warmth before you thaw the affected part further.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Once inside, gently remove gloves, rings, and any other constrictions, such as boots. Warm the affected part with your hands, in your lap, or continue to warm them in the casualty's armpits. Avoid rubbing the affected area because this can damage skin and other tissues.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Place the affected parts in tepid water, or lower than 104°F riesbandage</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Raise the affected limb to reduce swelling. An adult may take the recommended dose of acetaminophen, or her own pain medicine, and a child, the recommended dose of acetaminophen syrup (not aspirin). Take or send the casualty to the hospital</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="management-of-body-injuries">MANAGEMENT OF BODY INJURIES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management-of-body-injuries" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="fractures">FRACTURES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fractures" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>A break or crack in a bone is called a fracture. Considerable force is needed to break a bone, unless it is diseased or old. However, bones that are still growing are supple and may split, bend, or crack like a twig. A bone may break at the point where a heavy blow is received. Fractures may also result from a twist or a wrench (indirect force).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="recognition">RECOGNITION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recognition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>There may be:</p>
<ul>
<li>Deformity, swelling, and bruising at the fracture site</li>
<li>Pain and/or difficulty in moving the area</li>
<li>Shortening, bending, or twisting of a limb</li>
<li>Coarse grating (crepitus) of the bone ends that can be heard or felt (by casualty). Do not try to seek this. Signs of shock, especially if the thighbone or pelvis are fractured</li>
<li>Difficulty in moving a limb normally or at all (for example, inability to walk)</li>
<li>A wound, possibly with bone ends protruding (Treating an open fracture,</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="aims">AIMS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aims" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>To prevent movement at the injury site</li>
<li>To arrange transportation to the hospital, with comfortable support during transit</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="management">MANAGEMENT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Remember</p>
<ul>
<li>Keep RICE in mind, as a first aid treatment for all fractures, sprains and dislocations. RICE stands for Rest, Ice, Compression and Elevation.</li>
<li>Rest
Give plenty of rest to the immobilised limb. Move it as little as possible so that there is no strain.</li>
<li>Ice
Apply ice to the injured area. No heat treatment or massage should be given. Use an ice pack or wrap up some ice cubes in a damp towel and apply it to the injured area. You could also use anything frozen such as a packet of frozen peas.</li>
</ul>
<p>Do not massage the injured area, and don't apply any ointments like Iodex.</p>
<ul>
<li>Compression
Wrap up the injured area with a crepe bandage if possible, or use any clean, fresh cloth available. Wrap it as tight as is comfortable. However, ask the doctor before bandaging the area. This will relieve the pain somewhat.</li>
<li>Elevation
The injured limb should preferably be raised above the level of the heart. This could be done with the help of a pillow while sleeping.</li>
</ul>
<p>OPEN AND CLOSED FRACTURES</p>
<p>In an open fracture, one of the broken bone ends may pierce the skin surface, or there may be a wound at the fracture site. An open fracture carries a high risk of becoming infected. In a closed fracture, the skin above the fracture is intact. However, bones may be displaced</p>
<p>(unstable), causing internal bleeding and the casualty may develop shock.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="stable-and-unstable-fractures">STABLE AND UNSTABLE FRACTURES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#stable-and-unstable-fractures" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A stable fracture occurs when the broken bone ends do not move because they are not completely broken or they are impacted. Such injuries are common at the wrist, shoulder, ankle, and hip. Usually, these fractures can be gently handled without further damage.</p>
<p>In an unstable fracture, the broken bone ends can easily move. There is a risk that they may damage blood vessels, nerves, and organs around the injury. Unstable injuries can occur if the bone is broken or the ligaments are torn (ruptured). They should be handled carefully to prevent further damage.</p>
<p>Closed fracture The skin is not broken, although the bone ends may damage nearby tissues and blood vessels. Internal bleeding is a risk.</p>
<p>Open fracture Bone is exposed at the surface where it breaks the skin. The casualty may suffer bleeding and shock. Infection is a risk.</p>
<p>Stable fracture although the bone is fractured, the ends of the injury remain in place. The risk of bleeding or further damage is minimal.</p>
<p>Unstable fracture In this type of fracture, the broken bone ends can easily be displaced by movement or muscle contraction.</p>
